1948

Part One
ORTHOGRAPHY and PHONETICS

1. ALPHABET

1. The following alphabet was adopted by the «Rejaf Language Conferences» (1928).

a b c d d h e e g y i j k l m n nh ny
q o o p r t t h u w y.

A B C D D h E E G Y I J K L M N N h
N y Q O O P R T T h U W Y.

2. Vowels

The vowels have the French or Italian not the English sounds.

|  a | as in «far», | ä | as «e» in «wet»,  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  e | as in «pet», | ö | as «o» in «world».  |
|  e | as «a» in «Mary», | ï | as «i» in «bit»,  |
|  o | as in «door», | ë | as «e» in «get».  |
|  o | as «o» in «pot», |  |   |
|  i | as «ee» in «see», |  |   |
|  u | as «oo» in «foot». |  |   |

ä ë ï ö are centralised vowels and usually very short.

E. g.: wät (son), pl. weet; acuëk (twins); dit (bird), dit (big); dhöl (path, road), pl. dhol.

2 - Dinka Grammar.

2

Breathed vowels: This term means breathing out the vowels as they are enunciated and it occurs almost as frequently with vowels and diphthongs as our more common guttural pronunciation. For European students this breathed pronunciation is indicated throughout the grammar with the breathed vowels printed in bold types.

Examples are: koor akoor (it is small).
bay (quarrelsome), bay (marksman)
kap (to help to raise a burden), kap nya (to seduce a girl).

The examples indicate the necessity for taking particular care to indicate the difference between breathed and not breathed vowels.

## 3. Consonants

c as «ch» in «church». Avoid pronouncing «e» in a long chawn out manner such as in «lurch» (Engl.) but pronounce the «e» much as the «tch» in «twitch». At the end of a word it is sounded as if preceded by a short «i»: koe (people) sounds nearly «koic».

d t n are pronounced as in English but with the top of the tongue further back in the mouth.

dh th nh are pronounced with the top of the tongue pressed against the teeth.

y is a guttural sound near to the Arabic «gh». It is stronger before an «a» as in «yam» (thigh), otherwise feeble as in «yen» (I).

ny sounds like «gn» in Italian or French (signore, Boulogne).
g is like «ng» in «singing».

N. B. - a) Try to pronounce accurately and to distinguish between «piny» (earth, ground, down), and «piy» (to hear); «bany» (chiefs) and «bay» (marksman).

b) Learn to distinguish between final «n» and final «y», as in «nyan path» - a nice girl and «nyay path»; a nice crocodile; distinguish between «rin» (name) and «riy» (meat).

Final consonants have a feeble and uncertain pronunciation: final «c» sounds a bit like «j», «k» a bit like «g», «t» a bit like «d».

w and y are semivowels as in «wet» and «yet». They are not used in diphthongs nor after consonants nor at the end of a word. One writes for example: Mahal, Tuic, Abici, Mou. But one must write: Awan (a D. section, jakal), Mayar (a proper noun), amiyok (porcupine).

3

Doubled letters: doubled consonants have not been adopted. (An exception is «kadda»: sauce, with a marked double «d»).

Long vowels are marked by doubling the letter as in «raan» (person). Very short vowels have not been given any particular connotation.

Advice: It should be realised that a correct pronunciation of the sounds used in Dinka can be learned only with the assistance of a native. As soon as possible learn by heart whole phrases and short stories. Let native Dinkas repeat them (not read them) to you and imitate their pronunciation and accent until they are satisfied with your way of speaking.

## 2. Noun

Both the noun and the verb are usually monosyllables and consist of: consonant + vowel (or diphthong) + consonant (often missing after a diphthong).

E. g.: «tik», «raan», «rou» etc.

Nouns with more syllables are compound nouns. E. g.: «Madut», «Adut», etc. the first is a name for men, the second for women.

## A. Singular and Plural.

Nouns change from singular into plural in many different ways:

a) short vowels become long. E. g.: s. pal (knife), pl. paal (knives),
b) long vowels become short. E. g.: s. ciin (hand), pl. cin (hands).
c) many nouns change their vowel. E. g.:

s. baai (village), pl. beei (villages),
s. meth (child), pl. miith (children),
s. nhom (head), pl. nhiim (heads).

d) other nouns change their endings. E. g.:

s. yic (ear), pl. yith (ears);
s. rou (hippo), pl. roth (hippos);
s. nya (girl), pl. nyir (girls).

e) Sometimes the Dinka words for a noun in the singular and plural bear no obvious relation to each other. E. g.:

s. raan (person), pl. koe (people);
s. moe (man), pl. ror (men),
s. tik (woman), pl. diar (women);
s. wey (cow), pl. yok (cows).

Note: Nouns preceded by a numeral with the prefix «ka» are put in the plural; when without prefix, the noun is put in the singular.

4

E. g.: yok karou (two cows), weŋ thiaar (ten cows).

|  Numbers: | tok | rou | diak | yuan | dhiec | detem | dhorou | bet  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|   | 1 | 2 | 3 | 4 | 5 | 6 | 7 | 8  |
|  dhoyuan | thiaar | thiar ku rou, | thiar ku diak, | thiarrou, | thiarbet, |  |  |   |
|  9 | 10 | 12 | 13 | 20 | 80 |  |  |   |
|  buot (biantok), | buot karou. |  |  |  |  |  |  |   |
|  100 | 200 |  |  |  |  |  |  |   |

## B. Collective Noun:

This indicates a whole species or a large number of individuals. The vowel is changed as in the case of possessive suffixes shown later. E. g. weŋ (cow), weŋ (cattle). See also Pt. Two.

## Vocabulary

s. raan (person), pl. koe, s. bɛny (chief), pl. bany,
anoy, to have, awic, to want; *giric* (piaster),
s. ajith (hen), pl. ajiith, s. dhok (boy), pl. dhak,
s. gol (clan), pl. gal; s. baai (village), pl. bɛɛi, ku, and,
ato, ee, to be; thin, there; aliu, to be absent
yen, I; yin - you; yen, he, she, it.

## Exercise 1. A.

Yen awic ajiith karou. I want two hens.
Dhok anoy giric kadhiec. The boy has five piasters.
Yen ee raan gol bɛny. He is a man of the chief's clan.
Tik anoy ajith thiaar. The woman has ten hens.
Pal ato thin. There is a knife.
Raan anoy miith kadiak. The man has three children.
Dhak aliu baai. The boys are not at home.
Nyir ato baai. The girls are at home.
Beny anoy weŋ buot karou. The chief has 200 cows.
Aa dhak ku nyir bɛny. They are boys and girls of the chief.

## Exercise 1. B.

The man has two hands. Raan anoy ein karou.
I have a knife. Yen anoy pal.
The girls have clothes. Nyir anoy aleth.
The boy has 15 piasters. Dhok anoy giric thiar ku dhiec.
The chief has six children. Beny anoy miith kadetem.

5

The man is at home and the woman is not at home. The chief wants two cows. There are six boys and five girls.

Moc ato baai ku tik aliu baai. Beny awic yok karou. Dhak kadetem ato thin kw nyir kadhiec.

## 3

## Noun cases

wa, cok, war, kecam, acol, muoc,

my father; hunger; river, lake; food; I am calling, called; to present with.

pan geu, nyay, anek, yeen,

(qualified form of "baai"), town; crocodile; to be killed, tormented; me;

## 1) Objective case:

A noun or pronoun which is the direct object of a verb, lengthens its vowel.

E. g.: yen acol waa, muoc yeen kecam, give me food.

In these sentences «waa» and «yeen» are the objective case form of the nominatives «wa» and «yen».

Note: The lengthened vowel of the objective case is not usually indicated in writing, but in speaking the lengthened vowel should be stressed to convey the proper meaning.

## 2) Locative case:

One will notice when speaking with a Dinka that most place names are altered when referred to as «at a place», or moving «from a place». This altered form is the Locative case.

E. g.: Gagrial ee gen pan Rek, But: Wa ato Gagriel, or: Yen abo Gagriel,

G. is a town of the Rek. my father is at G. I am coming from G.

Rules for such vowel changes will be found in Part Two. Some very common nouns have irregular locatives.

E. g.: baai home, village. ma ato baai, Yen ato bei, war nyay ato wiir,

my mother is at home. I am going home. river. a crocodile is in the river.

Yok ato luek. The cattle are in the byre.

Roth kadhiec ato wiir. There are five hippos in the river.
Wa ato Madiey. My father is at Mading.
Diar ato baai. The women are at home.

My father is in the wood. Wa ato roor.
My mother is at home. Ma ato baai.
I am without money. Yen acin giric.
There is no fire in the byre. Mac aliu luek.
There is a hippo in the river. Rou ato wiir.
My father is at Gagrial. Wa ato Gagriel.
There is no crocodile in the river. Nyay aliu wiir.
The woman has a pot. Tik anoy tony.
The chief's children are at Kuajok. Miith beny ato Kuajook.

## 4. Qualified nouns

The ending and the vowel of a noun are liable to change when the noun is followed by another noun or by an adjective or by a possessive suffix.

7

a) A noun ending in certain consonants when followed by another noun or adjective or by an explanatory phrase changes its ending as follows:

|  p | changes into | m, | liep (tongue), | liem raan | = | a man's tongue.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  t | » | » | n, | yot (hut), | yon dit | = a big hut.  |
|  th | » | » | nh, | lueth (lie), | luenh rac | = a bad lie.  |
|  c | » | » | ny, | moc (man), | mony dit | = an old man.  |
|  k | » | » | y, | cok (hunger), | coy dit | = famine.  |
|   |   |   |   |   | Majoy e Lual, | = Majok son of L.  |

b) In similar circumstances a noun ending in a vowel takes an «n» added on to the end vowel.

E. g.: *nya* (girl), *nyan path* (a nice girl).

In similar circumstances a noun ending in a diphthong takes an «n» in place of the last vowel.

E. g.: *yeei.* (month), *yen war* (last month)
*rou* (hippo), *ron dit* (a big hippo)
*geu* (town), *gen dit* (chief town).

c) A noun with a possessive suffix changes often the vowel also. (See Part Two).

E. g.: *tik* (wife), *tieyde* (his wife);
*moc* (man), *muonyde* (her husband); *meth* (child), *manhdie* (my child);
*gol* (clan), *galde* (his clan); *pur* (boe), *piarde* (his hoe).

d) Nouns in their plural forms may be liable to vowel changes but are not liable to ending letter changes.

E. g.: *koc* (people), *kackie* (my people);
*miith* (children), *miethkie* (my children);
*puoth* (hearts), *piathkua* (our hearts).

## 5. A few words on Adjectives
*(See ch. 21 for a fuller explanation)*

Adjectives are put after a noun, e. g.: *raan dit* = a big man. They may be used like verbs and take verb particles (the particle «a» and the tense particles «ci» and «bi»).

E. g.: *yin adit* = you are big,
*tim aci diit* = the tree has grown.

Adjectives like verbs are also liable to vowel change, and the reader is directed to the succeeding chapter on Adjectives where the feature is dealt with more conveniently.

8

# 6. Verbs

The verb may have particles in front to indicate tenses and moods and may have suffixes to indicate the persons.

## Auxiliary verbs and Verb particles:

1) ee (sing.), aa (plur.) to be. It is also used as a verb particle to indicate a habitual action.

E. g. ee lei
aa mior e beny
raan dit ee koc muoc

it is an antelope.
they are the chief's bulls.
a great man gives presents to the people.

2) The Indicative particle «a-» put in front of a verb or tense-particle, has the meaning of a definite statement. Our present tense (of the indicative) corresponds to the verb form with «a-» in front.

E. g. I want something yen awic keday.
it has rained dey aci tueny.

Adjectives may also take the prefix «a-» and thereby become verbs.

E. g. yin apath you are nice, wck arac you (pl.) are bad.

3) The Past tense Particle «ci-» in the indicative takes the prefix «a-», viz. «aci-».

E. g.: koc aci jaal, the people have left.

4) The Future Tense Particle «bi» («abi» for the indicative).

E. g. Yen abi loo Wau I shall go to Wau.

5) The Habitual Particle «ye» (aye» for the indicative) indicates frequent actions or those continued permanently.

E. g. koc panda aye rap puur apei the people of our village cultivate much durra.

6) The Negative Particle «ci» for the present tense and for the past tense («aci» and «akee» for the indicative) and

«ci ye» (contracted into «cie») for the habitual tense («acie» for the indicative).

E. g.: Yen aci lo,
Mony akee jaal,
Koc acie luui,

I do not go.
the man has not left.
the people do not work.

9

# Vocabulary

Nhialic, God; luk, court, case; luui, to work;
piy, to hear, obey; *yukol*, today; *in* tiy, to travel;
warakol, yesterday; thou, to die; *nhiak*, tomorrow;
weu, money, goods; akol, day, time; wet, word, order.

# Exercise 3. A.

Ok abi wet beny piy. We shall obey the chief's order.
Bany abi luk looi nhiak. The chiefs will hold a court tomorrow.
Kek aa Jurcol ku ok aa Muonyjay. They are Jurs and we are Dinkas.
Manh e mony aci thou. This man's child has died.
Ok aye luui ku wek acie luui. We work and you do not work.
Wa akee ben yakol ab i ben nhiak. My father did not come today, he will come tomorrow.
Nhialic ato thin. There is a God.
Ok abi lo keny Wau. We shall make a journey to Wau.

# Exercise 3. B.

You are not Dinkas, you are Jurs. Wek acie Muonyjiey, wek aa Jurcol.
I have seen you. Yen aci yi tiy. *I have seen* ... *Yin akee tiy*
I did not ask money from you. Yen akee weu wic tene yin. *I have seen*
I shall work to day and tomorrow. Yen abi luui yakol ku nhiak yen
I shall travel. abi lo keny.
They will not obey the order. Kek aci *wet bi* piy.
The Jurs cultivate much durra. Jurcol aa rap puur apei./*weet*

N. B. Observe the position of the object, between the tense particle and the verb.

E. g. *yen aci yi tiy* I have seen you.

# 7. Verbs - Forms

1) The Main-Form of a verb is the one which has a long or half long vowel or diphthong. It is used alone in narrative or is preceded by the past, future or habitual particles (ci, bi or ye) and is not liable to changes.

2) The Indicative Form which takes the prefix «a» and which often corresponds to our present tense, has a short or very short vowel or diphthong, or is quite different from the main form. It is liable to vowel changes.

10

# Note on the Passive.

The construction of the passive will be explained in ch. 19. For the present it is sufficient to know, that when no agent is mentioned, the verb and the tense particles have the same form as in the active. The context and different intonation give the right meaning.

E. g.: yen acol manhde he is calling his son.
acol Lual he is called Lual.
yen acol he is called.

## Vocabulary

nhiaar, anhiar, to like, love; piy, apiy, to hear, obey;
jaal, ajal, to leave, go away; ben, abo, to come;
nok, anak, to kill, harm; lek, alek, to tell;
tau, atou, to put; tooc, atuc, to send;
lueel, aluel, to say; bei, abii, to bring, fetch;
emene, now; yic, true;
piu, (plur.) water. tim, tree;
cok, foot; under.

## Exercise 4. A.

Meth ee man nhiaar apei.
Yen abi jaal emene ku yin abi jaal nhiak.
Dhok abi piu bei tene yin.

Cok aci koc nok apei.
Benydit aci raan tooc yeu/pen
Yin akec wet yic luel.
Beny aci wet lek koc.
Koc abo yakol ku bany abi ben nhiak.
Raan aci nok.
Koc aci rou nok.

The child loves its mother much.
I am leaving now and you will leave tomorrow.
The boy will fetch water for you.
The people are very hungry.
The chief sent a man to the town.
You did not say the truth.
The chief gave an order to the people.
The people are coming today and the chiefs will come tomorrow.
A man has been killed.
The people have killed a hippo.

The children are very hungry.

## 8. Possessive suffixes

They are as follows:

|  Singular | Plural |  | Singular | Plural |   |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  -die | -kie | my, | -da | -kua | our,  |
|  -du | -ku | your, | -dun | -kun | your,  |
|  -de | -ke | his (her) | -den | -ken | their.  |

As pointed out in ch. 4, these suffixes cause in most cases a vowel and an ending change in the noun to which they are joined. The rules for such changes will be found in Part Two.

Note: the irregular forms of:

baai (village), pan path a nice village.
panda our village,
beeiken (or baaiken) their village.
meth (child, 'son), manhde his child,
mielhke his children.

## Vocabulary

cool, acol to call, rin (pl.) name, muor (miar-) bull,
dhieth to give birth, yep axe, toy spear, fight,
(also noun: clan) rac bad, ugly, cok foot, footprint,
path good, nice, koor lion, lei, pl. lai, game;
cath to walk, ke,kek, with.

## Exercise 5. A.

Nyiandie acol Abuk. My daughter is called Abuk.
Manhde acol Dut. His son is called Dut.
Weydie apath. My cow is nice.
Manhdu anhiar weyde. Your son loves his cow.
Tieyde aci manh moc dhieth. His wife has given birth to a little boy.
Yen aci lai tiy. I have seen game.
Rienke acol Madut. He is called Madut.

Koor aci wey panda cam.
The lion has eaten a cow in our village.

Panden arac, cok ato thin.
Their country is bad, there is famine.

Tik acath kek manhde.
The woman is walking with her child.

Manhdie aci thou warakol.
My child has died yesterday.

Yen abi miardie nok.
I shall kill my bull.

## Exercise 5. B.

Our clan is called Paliec.
Dhienhda acol Paliec.

People of your clan are in our village.
Koc dhienhdun ato panda.

Your people are bad, they want a fight.
Kacku arac, kek awic toy.

My bull is big and your bull is small.
Miardie adit ku miardu akoor.

The women have gone to their homes.
Diar aci loo baaiken.

The mother loves her children.
Man ee miethke nhiaar.

My spear is in the byre.
Taydie ato luck.

Your axe is bad.
Yemdu arac.

I saw the footprints of a lion.
Yen aci cok koor tiy.

## 9. Relationship Terms

There are no general abstract terms like «father», «mother», «uncle» etc. but the Dinka terms always mean the «father», «mother», «uncle», etc. of somebody. The terms for father and mother have already been shown in Exercise 2. Only a few other terms will be shown here, for the rest consult Part Two.

|  wamaath | my brother, | nyankai | my sister,  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  wamuth | your brother, | nyankui | your sister,  |
|  wamenh | his (her) brother, | nyanken | his (her) sister,  |
|  wamathkie | my brothers, | nyirakai | my sisters,  |
|  wamathku | your brothers, | nyiraku | your sisters,  |
|  wamathke | his (her) brothers, | nyirake | his (her) sisters,  |
|  wamathda | our brother, | nyankaida | our sister,  |
|  wamathkua | our brothers, | nyirakua | our sisters.  |

## Vocabulary

|  lil | grassplain, swamp, | pur | hoe (or verb 'to till') | rap | durra,  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  nyuom | simsim, | dom | field, | pon | to weed,  |
|  dey | rain, | dey | atueny it rains, | apei | very much.  |

## 10. Sex indication

A few animals have special terms for the different sexes.

- muor (pl. mior) bull, thil (pl. thiel) cob, female cob,
- wey (» yok) cow, gar (» gaar) male cob,
- dou (» dau) heifer, maluith. (» miluith) young male cob.

The sex of other animals is indicated by the words thon (pl. thon) male (or muor), and yuot (pl. yut) female put after the animal's name.

E. g. akoon ē muor bull elephant lioness.

In cattle names the prefix ma- indicates bulls and the prefix a- indicates cows.

14

## II. Prepositions
*(Nouns, pronouns and verbs acting as such)*

**Tene**
«to» (when referring to a distant person otherwise omitted).
E. g. *yen abi weu yek tene yen*
I shall give him money.

«from» (a person).
E. g. *yen aci wet piŋ tene mony*
I heard the news from this man.

**kam**
between, among.
E. g. *yen adit kam koe kok*
he is the oldest among the others.

Possessive suffixes take the place of personal pronouns.
E. g. *yega kamkun*
which of you?

**rin**
for, on behalf, for the sake of, by reason of.
E. g. *rin tuany*
by reason of sickness.

*jam e rienkua*
speak for us.

**Yet**
until, as far as.
E. g. *ok abi caath yet Wau*
we shall walk as far as Wau.

*ok abi niin yet dhien ajith*
we shall sleep until cock-crow.

**ke (kek)**
with, and.
E. g. *wa ke ma*
my father and my mother.

*mony kek tieyde*
this man with his wife.

The following terms for parts of the human body, when put after a noun, take the place of our prepositions of place.
*nhom* (pl. *nhiim*) on, in face of.

15

E. g. *tim nhom*
on the tree.
*kɔc nhiim*
in face of the people.

*cok* (pl. *cok*)
under, at the foot of.

E. g. *kur cok*
at the foot of the rock (or hill).

*kou* (pl. *koth*)
behind.

E. g. *yot kou*
behind the hut.

*ic* (pl. *yic*)
locative of *ʒac* = belly, in, inside.

E. g. *wutic*
in the cattle camp.

*cok cien*
after.

E. g. *luoi cok cien*
after the work.

*nhom tuey*
before.

E. g. *luoi nhom tuey*
before the work.

*yin abi caath koc nhiim tuey eben*
you will walk in front of the people.

## 12. Pronouns

### 1) Personal Pronouns.

|  yen | I, | ok | we, | a (or "ya") | o (or "yo")  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  yin | you, | wek | you, | yi | we  |
|  yen | he, she, it, | kek | they, | ye | ke  |

Remember ch. 3, Objective Case, in which the vowel is lengthened (yɛɛn).

*Note:* the personal pronouns of the third person singular and plural are often omitted where we put them.

E. g. *aci jaal*
he has gone away.

### 2) Demonstrative Pronouns and Adjectives.

|  kene | (pl. kaka) | this. | kɛnɛ | (pl. kakui) | that.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  "-e" | (pl. -ka) | this. |  |  |   |

16

Both are suffixed and «-e» causes ending change in the preceding pronoun.
E. g. *tik* woman, *tiye* this woman.

### 3) Interrogative Pronouns.

|  ya? | who? | ye yin ya? | who are you?  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  yo? | what? | ye yo? | what is it? why?  |

### 4) Indefinite Pronouns.

|  yek | somebody, | keday (pl. kay) | something.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  dä (pl. kok) | other, | det (pl. kok) | other (different).  |
|  kedhie | all, both. |  |   |

### 5) Reflexive Pronouns.

|  rot or: rot | self. |  |   |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  E. g.: | kony rot | help | yourself.  |
|   | kuonyke rot | help | yourselves.  |
|  guop | self (subject), | plur. opec | ourselves,  |
|   |  | wepec | yourselves,  |
|   |  | kepec | themselves.  |
|  E. g.: | yin guop | you | yourself,  |
|   | kek kepec | they | themselves.  |

## Exercise 7. A.

|  Monye acol Col. | This man is called Col.  |
| --- | --- |
|  Yin ee wa, kony yeen. | You are my father, help me.  |
|  Col wur ya? | What is the name of your father?  |
|  Yeyo wic? Yen awic weu tene yin. | What do you want? I want money from you.  |
|  Kocka aa kackie. | These people are my people.  |
|  Yen awic keday. Yiek ya kene. | I want something. Give me this.  |
|  Yen guop yen abi loo tene yin. | I myself I shall go to you.  |
|  Awic det. Kok aliu. | He wants another one. There are no others.  |

## Exercise 7. B.

|  What does he want from me? | Yeyo wic tene yen?  |
| --- | --- |
|  He does not want anything, he is just walking about. | Acin keday wic, acath ēpath.  |

17

Give me another one.
There is no other.
What is your name?
My name is Bol.
You yourselves were absent.
Some boys are at home and the others are in the wood.
Only the women are weeding.
They all went away.

Yiek ya det.
Det aliu.
Col yin ya?
Yen acol Bol.
Wek wepec ku wek aliu thin.
Dhak kok ato baai ku dhak kok ato roor.
Diar kepec kek apun.
Kek aci jaal kedhie.

## 13. Negative sentences

As shown in ch. 5. kec is the negative particle for the past tense and ci for the other tenses.

If there is an object, it is put after kec or ci.

E. g. yin akec wet yic lueel
yin aci weu bi yok

you did not speak the truth.
you will not get the money.

The habitual particle ye can be contracted with the negative particle, ci-ye becomes cie.

E. g. wal kene acie koc nok this medicine does not harm people.

**Important.** The negative ci, in default of a tense particle (bi, ye) is followed by a verb with the indicative (shorter) form. The tense particle ci (past tense) is followed by a verb with main form (longer). The negative verb is less accentuated than the positive one and less than its particle ci.

E. g. yen aci loo
yen aci lo
aci ben
aci bo
aci koc nok
aci koc nik

I have gone.
I do not go.
he has come.
he does not come.
it has killed people.
it does not harm the people.

## Vocabulary

adhuk to return, aloi, to do, alui to work,
agat, got to write, apioc to teach, athor letter,
ruon (pl. run) year, yeruon this year, peei moon, month.

## Exercise 8. A.

Ok abi dhuk nhiak.
Yin akec luui warakol.
Yen aci lui yakol, yen aci luui warakol.

We shall return tomorrow.
You did not work yesterday.
I do not work today, I have worked yesterday.

3 - Dinka Grammar.

18

Dhok agat athor.
Cok aci ya nak yakol.
Yin akec miith piooc apath.

The boy is writing a letter.
Hunger does not torment me today.
You have not taught the children well.

Mony aci toy looi.
Mony aci luui ruon tok ku pei kadiak.
Yen aci jal.
Wa aci jaal ku abi dhuk nhiak.

The man has been fighting.
The man has worked one year and three months.
I do not leave.
My father has left and will return tomorrow.

## Exercise 8. B.

The man does not like you.
He will not come now, he will come another day.
The chief has not come; the other people have come.
I am not hungry now.
I did not speak a lie, i tell the truth.
We do not work today.
We shall cultivate much durra and we shall not suffer hunger this year.

Monye aci yi nhiar.
Aci bo emene, abi ben akol da.
Beny akec ben, koe kok aci ben.
Cok aci ya nak emene.
Yen akec lueth lueel, yen aluel wet yic.
Ok acie luui yakol.
Ok abi rap puur apei ku cok aci ok bi nok yeruon.

## 14. Interrogative

In the interrogative the particle a- is omitted.
The second person of the singular has a very short e- as suffix.
The three persons of the plural have the suffixes: -ku, -ke, -ke.

Examples:
luie? yen alui
lui? alui
luiku? ok abi luui
luoike? ok alui
luike?
to wur baai?
wa ato baai?
jale? yen aci jal

are you working? yes I am.
is he working? yes he is.
shall we work? we shall work.
do you work? we do.
do they work? they do.
is your father at home?
my father is at home.
do you leave? I do not leave.

In the interrogative of non compound tenses several verbs are liable to vowel change. Cp. Part Two, ch. 23.

E. g. luoike? ok alui
nienke? ok aci nin

are you working? we are.
are you sleeping? we are not.

19

Intransitive verbs have in answers the full verb forms (not the suffixes: ku, ke, ke). Transitive verbs have also in answers the shortened forms.

E. g. nhiarke mordun? do you love your mother?
mada anhiarku we love our mother.

In compound tenses a pronoun subject is contracted with the tense particle as shown in ch. 16. Transitive verbs have such contracted forms also in the answers. Intransitive verbs have in the answers the full form.

E. g. cak pip? bave you understood?
acuk pip we have understood (it).
bak jaal? will you (pl.) leave?
ok abi jaal we shall leave.

## Negative Interrogative

E. g. ci bo? is'nt he coming?
aci bo he does not come.
ci beny bi ben yakol? will the chief not come today?
aci bi ben he will not.
koc kec wetdie pip? did the people not obey my order?
wetdu akecke pip they did not obey your order.

## 15. The imperative (Command and prohibition)

1) The imperative form of a verb is the same as the interrogative form. They are distinguished only by their intonation.

E. g. jäle go (sing.),
jalke go (pl.), jielku let us go.

2) Prohibitions are formed with the help of the auxiliary duk (pl. 1. duokku, 2. duokke).

E. g. duk cuer do not steal.
duokke koc let do'nt insult people.
duokku wada jor let us not annoy our father.
duokke miir ye nok do'nt (pl.) kill giraffes.

After duk the verb has the indicative or the habitual form.

## Vocabulary

reer, arɛɛr to stay, remain, lat, alɛt to insult,
cam, acam to eat, attack, bar (pl. bak) come,
tuany sickness, to be sick, kerac evil, snake,

20

jok
joor, ajor,
pakier yo?
kuin

spirit, disease,
to annoy,
what clan?
porridge.

neen
bak
dä (or: dē)
ēpath,

when?
to be well, appear,
how? where?
without reason,
«saket» (arab.)

He did not hear my case.

## 16. Contractions of pronouns and verb particles

1) In questions and answers a subject pronoun is contracted with the tense particles ci, bi, and ye as follows.

yen aci, abi, aye are contracted to: aca, aba, ayaa,
yin » » » » » aca, aba, ayaa,
yen » » » » » aci, abi, ayee,
ok » » » » acuk, abuk, ayeku,
wek » » » » acak, abak, ayake,
kek » » » » acik, abik, ayek.

N. B. In questions the prefix a- is omitted.

21

E. g. *cak tiy? acuk tiy*
*bak ben nhiak? ok abi ben*
have you seen (it)? we have.
will you come tomorrow? we shall come.

**Ben** is an intransitive verb and therefore in the answer the particle **bi** is not contracted with the pronoun **ok**.

2) If the *pronoun* is the *object* and a singular, its shortened form is put after the tense particle and may be contracted with it as follows.

*aci ya*
*or: acaa, aci yi*
*or: acii, aci ye*
*or: acee, abii yi*
*» abii, abi ye* » *abee.*

Examples:
*yin acaa lat*
*wur abii muk*
*beny abi ok muooo*
you have insulted me.
your father will care for you.
the chief will give us something.

## Exercise 10. A.

*Ca wamaath tiy geu?*
*Wamuth aca tiy ku areer apath.*
*Ba jaal neen?*
*Yen abi jaal yakol guop.*
*Ci koc luui gekic?*
*Koc aci luui.*
*Ba luoydie piy yakol?*
*Luoydu aba piy.*
*Ba ya muooo alath?*
*Yin aba muooo alanh path.*
*Yin acaa muooo, yin aca nhiaar apei*

Did you see my brother in the town?
I saw your brother and he is well.
When will you leave?
I shall leave this very day.
Did the people work on the road?
The people did work.
Will you hear my case today?
I shall hear your case.
Will you give me some clothing?
I shall give you some nice clothing.
You have made me a present, I love you very much.

Did you obey my order? yes I did.
You have helped me very much.
You gave me a lot of trouble.
Will you return today? I shall return.
Do you cultivate simsim in your country?
We do.
Have you shot a male cob?
I shot one.
Have you seen a crocodile in the river?
Yes, we saw one.
I am very hungry.

*Ca wetdie piy? wetdu aca piy.*
*yin acaa kony apei.*
*Wek acaa joor apei.*
*Ba dhuk yakol? yen abi dhuk.*
*Yake nyuom puur pandun?*

*Nyuom ayeku puur.*
*Ca gar moc?*
*Aaca moc.*
*Cak nyaq tiy wiir?*

*Nyaq acuk tiy.*
*Cok acaa nok apei.*

3) The negative particle ci and a subject pronoun are contracted as follows.

|  yen | aci | becomes | acaa | I am (do) not,  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  yin | » | » | acii | you are (do) not,  |
|  yen | » | » | acii | he is (does) not,  |
|  ok | » | » | acuk | we are (do) not,  |
|  wek | » | » | acak | you are (do) not,  |
|  kek | » | » | acik | they are (do) not.  |

The negative particle for the past tense akec and subject pronouns are contracted as follows.

|  akεc. | akεc. | akεc. | akeεku. | akeεke. | akeεke.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  I did not, | you did not, | he did not, | we did not, | you did not, | they did not.  |

Note. In order to distinguish phrases like yin acaa nhiaar (you have loved me) and yin acaa nhiar (I do'nt like you) remember ch. 13. Negative Sentences.

Have they stolen your money?  *Cik weuku kuaal?*
They have stolen it.  *Aacik kuaal.*
Do you not work on the road?  *Cak lui gek?*
We do not work today.  *Ok aci lui yakol.*
Do boys drink merissa in your country?  *Ye dhak mou dek pandun?*
They do not drink merissa.  *Mou acik ye dek.*
The man has insulted you, did you not hear?  *Mony acii lat, kec piy?*

## 17. Narrative

A narrative begins with a sentence whose verb is preceded by the particle ē plur. aa. The letter takes plural forms of pronoun suffixes (aao, aawe, aake) and they may be joined to the tense particle ci. (ēci, aao ci, aawe ci, aake ci).

E. g.  *Thzerwar koc karou aake ci lo keny*  long ago two people set out on a journey.
*Yonthzer beny ēlo thin*  long ago there was a chief.

The following sentences of the narrative are introduced by the conjunctions go or ku, both meaning and.

Go (=and) is followed by a noun, subject or object, and then by the verb in its main form. In the negative it has the indicat. form preceded by ci.

E. g.  *Ruon war wa ēci lo keny*  last year my father made a journey and found a lion on the road.
*Go man ci bo bei yot*  and his mother did not come out from the hut.

Ku (=and or: but) is used when the following event is strictly connected with the preceding one. Ku is followed by the verb which has the indicat. form.

E. g.  *Mony ēci weyde lo wic go*  this man was seeking his cow and anyway *yok ku nāk*  found a buffalo and killed it.

## 18. Passive

Two forms have to be distinguished viz. when the agent causing the action is mentioned and when it is not mentioned.

1) When the agent is mentioned verbs without tense particle are accentuated and may undergo vowel change. In compound tenses the

24

vowel of the tense particle or the negative particle ci (followed by the agent) is lengthened.

E. g. cok anak yeen
Yen anek cok
Yen acii cok nok
Yen acul waa
Yen acool wa
Yen acii wa cool

hunger kills me.
I am killed by hunger.
I have been killed by hunger.
I am calling my father.
I am called by my father.
I have been called by my father.

2) When the agent is not mentioned the verb form is the same as in the active.

E. g. yen acol
raan aci nok

I am called.
a man has been killed.

The passive meaning is understood by the lack of an object needed by a transitive verb in the active voice. E. g. yen acol cannot mean I am calling (which in Dinka is worded yen acol) -acol needs an object when it has an active meaning. There is none here and therefore acol has a passive meaning viz. is called.

## Exercise 12. A.

Meth anhier man.
Joydie acii kuac cam.
Weydu aciem nyay.
Wek abii cok nok yeruon.
Yen acii wa nhiar, or: yen aci nhiar e wa.
Banyda anhier jay eben.
Moguak ayee Jurcol puur ku acie puur e koe liil.
Yen amen kackie.

The child is loved by his mother.
My dog has been eaten by a leopard.
Your cow is eaten by a crocodile.
You will suffer hunger this year.
I am not liked by my father.
Our chief is liked by the whole tribe.
Groundnuts are cultivated by the Jurs and are not cultivated by the people of the swamp.
I am disliked by my people.

## Exercise 12. B.

My hens have been eaten by a hyena.
This man is hated by all the people.
I have been asked by the chief.
Milk is liked by all children.
The groundnuts have all been eaten by the boys.

Ajietskie acii ayui cam.
Mony acii koe maan eben.
Yen acii beny thieec.
Ca anhier miith eben.
Atom acii dhak cam eben.

25

# 19. Verb - changes

1) As pointed out in ch. 7, the verb has a main form with longer vowel or diphthong and a shorter form for the simple indicative. In some verbs their vowels are quite different.

Also the passive voice may cause vowel change as shown in the preceding ch. 18, 1.

a also in diphthongs changes to e. E. g.:

|  Active | Passive (with agent mentioned)  |
| --- | --- |
|  aman (to hate) | amen (to be hated),  |
|  anyai (to take away) | anyei (to be taken away),  |
|  akual (to steal) | akuel (to be stolen).  |

2) A transitive verb used without object (thereby becoming in-trans.) changes the vowel or the vowel will get a breathed pronounciation. A final I may become a t. Examples are:

|  Transitive form: | Intransitive form:  |
| --- | --- |
|  yen acol raan I am calling a person, | yen acol I am calling.  |
|  yen aket wak I am singing a song, | yen akit I am singing.  |
|  yen aloi kene I do this, | yen alui I am working.  |
|  tik athal kuin the woman is cooking porridge. | tik athat the woman is cooking.  |
|  yen anhiar kene I like this, | yen anhier I like.  |
|  koc apur rap the people are cultivating durra. | koc apur the people are cultivating.  |

3) An indirect complement may equally affect the verb form.

Examples:
wa acaa tooc my father has sent me.
wa acaa tuoc yiin my father has sent me to you.
mony aci mac kuany the man took the rifle (became a soldier).
yen aci kene kueny bei I chose this.
kek aci muor nok they killed a bull.
kek aci muor nak yath they sacrificed a bull to the totem.
yen abi keny cool I shall pay the debt.
yen abi cuol yiin I shall pay you.
ka-aca puol I desisted from it.
aca pal piny I have forgiven.

4) The inverted construction (first verb and then the subject) used in dependent clauses, may equally cause vowel change. In compound

26

tenses the tense particles ci and bi become cii and bii, the negative ci becomes cii.

Examples: yen ajil
I am leaving.
wen jiel yen
when I left.
yen anhiar yiin
I like you.
rin nhier yen yiin
because I like you.
yin aci kedie kuaal
you have stolen my belongings.
rin cii yin kedie kuaal
because you have stolen my belongings.
yin aci lui apath
you do not work well.
yeyo cii yin lui apath?
why do you not work well?

5) In questions and answers, in the imperative and in other shortened phrases not mentioning a subject or an object the verbs are liable to vowel changes in some persons of the singular and the plural. Only a few examples will be given in the following exercise to illustrate these rather complicated rules. For the rest consult Table VIII in Part Two.

## Vocabulary

kat to run away, tiit, atit to wait, await.
lok to wash muoth to greet
pial, apuōl to be light or healthy.

Men yiin? amen.
does he hate you? he does.
Miathke raan dit. aamuothku.
greet the elder, we greet him.
Ku ket.
and he ran away.
Thuou
may I die.
Tiet yi? tit ya.
shall I await you? await me.
Lok alethkie; alak.
wash my clothes; I wash them.
Pialke gup? ok apuōl gup
are you well? yes we are.

## 20. Conjugations

### I. WITHOUT CONTRACTION OF PRONOUN AND VERB PARTICLE.

The verb and its particle (tense particle and negative particle) remain the same in all persons of the singular and the plural. Therefore only the first person is put in the table below. The yen may be replaced by every one of the other persons (yin, yen, ok, wek, kek).

27

# Intransitive verbs

## Present tense

|  Indicative | yen alui | I am working  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|   | yen aci lui | I do'nt work  |
|  Commands | lui(e) | work  |
|  (Questions) |  | (do you work?)  |
|   | luiku | let us work  |
|   |  | (shall we work?)  |
|   | luoike | work (pl.)  |
|   |  | (do you work?)  |
|   | luike? | do they work?  |
|  Prohibition: | duk lui | do'nt work  |
|   | dukku lui | let us not work  |
|   | duokke lui | do'nt work (pl.)  |

## Past tense

|  Indicative | yen ani luui | I have worked  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|   | yen akec luui | I did not work  |

## Future tense

|  Indicative | yen abi luui | I shall work  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|   | yen aci bi luui | I shall not work  |

## Habitual

|  yen aye luui | I work  |
| --- | --- |
|  yen acie luui | I do not work  |

## Transitive verbs (active voice)

### Present tense

|  Indicative | yen anhiar raan | I like the person  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|   | yen aci raan nhiar | I do'nt like the person  |
|  Commands | nhiar(e) raan | like the person  |
|  (Questions) |  | (do you like the person?)  |
|   | nhiarku raan | let us like the p.  |
|   |  | (shall we like the p.)  |
|   | nhiarke raan | like (pl.) the person  |
|   |  | (do you like the person?)  |
|  Prohibition | duk raan nhiar | do'nt like the person  |
|   | dukku raan nhiar | let us not like the person  |
|   | duokke raan nhiar | do'nt like the person  |

28

## Past tense

Indicative
yen aci raan nhiaar I liked the person
yen akec raan nhiaar I did not like the person

## Future tense

Indicative
yen abi raan nhiaar I shall like the person
yen aci raan bi nhiaar I shall not like the person

## Habitual

Indicative
yen aye raan nhiaar I like the person
yen acie raan nhiaar I do'nt like the person.
(or: yen aci raan ye nhiaar)

## Transitive verbs (passive voice)

### INDICATIVE

#### Present tense

yen anhiar I am liked
yen anhier raan I am liked by the person
yen aci nhiar I am not liked
yen aci nhiar ë raan I am not liked by the person

#### Past tense

yen aci nhiaar I was liked
yen acii raan nhiaar I was liked by the person
yen akec nhiaar I was not liked
yen akec nhiaar ë raan I was not liked by the person

#### Future tense

yen abi nhiaar I shall be liked
yen abii raan nhiaar I shall be liked by the p.
yen aci bi nhiaar I shall not be liked
yen aci bi nhiaar ë raan I shall not be liked by the person

29

# Habitual

yen aye nhiaar
yen ayee raan nhiaar
yen acie nhiaar
yen acie nhiaar e raan

I am liked
I am liked by the person
I am not liked
I am not liked by the person

## II. WITH CONTRACTION OF PRONOUN AND VERB PARTICLE

### Intransitive verbs

#### Present tense

caa lui? do I not work?
cii lui? do you not work?
cii lui? does he not work?
cuk lui? do we not work?
cak lui? do you not work?
cik lui? do they not work?

#### Future tense

ba luui? shall I work?
ba luui? will you work?
bi luui? will he work?
buk luui? shall we work?
bak luui? will you work?
bik luui? will they work?

#### Past tense

ca luui? did I work?
ca luui? did you work?
ci luui? did he work?
cuk luui? did we work?
cak luui? did you work?
cik luui? did they work?

#### Habitual

yoa luui? do I work?
yaa luui? do you work?
yee luui? does he work?
yeku luui? do we work?
yake luui? do you work?
yeke luui? do they work?

I did not work

### Active voice

yen ēnāk lai

kek aake nāk lai
kek aake ci (-ye) lai nok

I killed game,
I killed game,
they killed game,
they killed game.

# Passive voice

|  yen ē lāt | I was insulted,  |
| --- | --- |
|  yen ēci (ēye) lat | I have been insulted.  |
|  yen ēyee koc kony | I was helped by the people,  |
|  yen ēcii koc kony, | I have been helped by the people.  |
|  kek aake lāt | they were insulted,  |
|  kek aake ci (ye) lat | they have been insulted.  |
|  kek aake yee koc kony. | they were helped by the people.  |
|  kek aake cii koc kony. | they have been helped by the people..  |

# Periods introduced by «GO»

|  gua loo | and I went, | gua nyay mooc | and I shot a crocodile.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  go loo | and you went, | go nyay mooc | and you shot a crocodile..  |
|  go loo | and he went, | go nyay mooc | and he shot a crocodile.  |
|  goku loo | and we went, | goku nyay mooc | and we shot a crocodile.  |
|  guoke loo | and you went, | guoke nyay mooc | and you shot a crocodile.  |
|  goke loo | and they went, | goke nyay mooc | and they shot a crocodile..  |
|  gua ci lo | and I did not go,  |
| --- | --- |
|  gua nyay ci nāk | and I did not kill the crocodile.  |
|  gua cok nōk | and I was tormented by hunger,  |
|  gua cok ci nēk | I was not tormented by hunger..  |
|  goke cok nōk | and they were tormented by hunger,  |
|  goke cok ci nēk | they were not tormented by hunger.  |

# 21. Adjectives

1) Adjectives are words qualifying a noun and are in Dinka: put after it,

e. g. raan path a nice person.

They may cause an ending change in the preceding noun as shown in ch. 4, dhok (boy), dhoy rac (a bad boy).

2) Adjectives may be constructed like verbs and take verb particles (a-, aci, abi) and they may be liable to vowel change.

E. g. pan path
panda apath
baai aci piath
yin aci path acin

a nice country
our country is nice
the country has improved
you are not nice at all.

3) Comparative. There is a special term for «better» (yuen). The other comparatives are expressed by the conjunctions tene and kam (for plural) put before the second term.

32

yen adit tene yin
yeya dit kamken?
I am older than you.
who is the eldest among them?

4) The absolute superlative is indicated by the adverbs: apei, apeidit, and others. Cp. Pt. Two.
E. g. monytui adhey apei
that man is very kind

5) There is no word corresponding to the English too. The positive form of the adjective is used and the context gives the right meaning.
E. g. alath adit (or: adit tene yen)
the coat is too big (for me),

6) Verb forms used like adjectives. Verbs also in compound tenses, without the prefix a are put after a noun like adjectives and the noun is liable to ending change like in the case of an adjective.

E. g. lan koc cam
manh pioc
raan ci mac
a man-eater
pupil
prisoner

## 22. Auxiliary verbs

The principal verb is in Dinka frequently accompanied by one or more auxiliary verbs which act as adverbs or as English verbs like to get in «to get tired» or to do in «I did not see him».

The principal verb preceded by an auxiliary verb has the main form.

E. g. aci jal ben
Ku ben nok
and then he came
and he hurt him again.

The most common auxiliary verbs are:

lo, alo (to go to). Yen alo niin
jal, ajol (then, at last). Ku jol beny jam
ben, aben (again). Koc aci ben dhuk
lac, aloc (soon, quickly). Lacke luui
kay, akoy (first). Mony aci kay yeet
lok, alok (afterwards). Koc ci lok ben

I am going to sleep.
and then spoke the chief.
the people have returned again.
work quickly.
this man arrived first.
the people which came afterwards.

END OF PART ONE

# Part Two

## 1. Alphabet

### Open and narrow vowels.

(e, e, o, o) have to be properly distinguished if one wants to be understood easily by the Dinka. Different pronunciation gives the word a different meaning. Some very common names are distinguished only in this way.

E. g. Majok and Majok, Aken and Aken.

In order to avoid the inconvenience of special letters the distinction could be indicated also by an accent on the open vowel (e, o).

### Breathed vowels.

Their pronunciation by breathing out has to be learned with the help of a Dinka taking as examples similar words distinguished by whether their vowel is breathed out or not stressed by breathing.

Examples:
|  akɔi | small pot | lɛi | antelope.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  akɔl | sun | akɔn | elephants.  |
|  dit | bird | dit | big.  |
|  koor | small | koor | lion.  |
|  manh koor | a little child | manh koor | lion cub  |
|  akɔɔn adit ku | koor akoor. |  |   |

The guttural sound y should not be pronounced strongly in words such as yen (l), yɔc (to buy). It is more marked in front of a as in yam (thigh), yal (pit-trap), yac (to carry).

The dental consonants (nh, dh, th) have a more dental pronunciation in the eastern tribes which in pronouncing them push the tongue beyond the upper incisors. The Rek and especially the Malual give them a less dental pronunciation, especially when finals. E. g. The Malual say dhiet (to give birth) and the other tribes dhieth.

Final c k p th have an uncertain and weak pronunciation. It is the convention to use only the mute forms as finals. The real pronunciation depends also on the single individuals who speak and on their age. Often one would prefer to write for instance Dud and tab for Dut and tap.

4 - Dinka Grammar.

34

## Double vowels.

Remarkably long vowels should be written with two letters but it has become usual in many cases also to write long vowels with a single letter especially if there is a specific reason. For instance in order to avoid confusion between a long common vowel, and a long breathed vowel (this breathing feature is not marked in ordinary Dinka texts) or if the right meaning and therefore the right pronunciation is easily understood from the context. E. g. one should write *koor* (small, young) plur. *kor* and *akoor* = to be young, but nearly all people especially the natives write *kor* in the singular and in the plural and *akor*. It serves to distinguish it more easily from *koor* (lion).

## 2. Nouns

### Singular and Plural.

There are nouns which have only a plural form and which are constructed like plurals, e. g. *piu* (water), *piuke* (its w.) *rin* (name), *ričnkie* (my name). In the eastern dialects *rin* is used like a singular e. g. *rindu* (your name).

*Piu* (water) has also another plural form *piou* or *pieu* to indicate a great quantity of water or a flood.

*Raan* (person) has no plural form, *koc* (people) is used in its place. Nouns ending in a diphthong drop the second vowel and take *th* in its place.

E. g. kou pl. koth seeds rou pl. roth hippo
riai pl. rieth canoe gcu pl. gcth town.

Vowel changes in the plural: a changes in the plural frequently into *c* or into *e*.

E. g. kat pl. ket frame
kuac pl. kucc leopard
mac pl. mcc fire, fire-arm
ray pl. rey tomb.

Nevertheless even for a and still less for the other vowels a general rule can not be laid down for vowel changes. Many examples will be found in table I. after ch. 5.

### Collective Nouns.

Their form is derived from that form which a noun has when followed by a possessive suffix, but the vowel is lengthened and the ending has its original form.

Examples: dhok boy dhaydie my boy collective dhaak boys
moc man muonydie my husband collective muoc men.

35

Irregular collective nouns:

tik woman pl. diar coll. dior or titek women.
nya girl pl. nyir coll. nyal girls.

Exercise 14.

Yen anoy yok kadiak. I have three cows.
Beny anoy miith kadhiec. The chief has five children.
Raan anoy wey thiarrou. The man has 20 cows.
Koc aliu baai. The people are not at home.
Dhok anoy pal. The boy has a knife.
Diar ato baai ku ror aliu baai. The women are at home and the men are not at home.
Raan anoy cin karou. A man has two hands.
Nyir anoy aleth. The girls have clothes.
Roth kadhiec ato wiir. There are five hippos in the river.
Tik areer baai, moc aci lo keny. The woman is at home, the man is on a journey.
Yen aca ajiith kabet yoooc. I have bought eight hens.
Ee wey acie lai. (They) are cows not game.
Dior panda aciey buoy thok ku buoy thi. The women of our country wear skins of goat and of female cob.
Puor adek wiir. Waterbuck are drinking at the river.
Piar karou aa thon ku kok aa yut. Two waterbuck are males and the others are females.
Ye raan kiith yo kene? What a crowd is this?

3.

Cases

1) Objective case.

This is formed by accentuating or lengthening the vowel of a noun when it is the object to which a verb refers.

E. g.: yen I, yeen me.

The Dinka spelling rules have not adopted the distinction in writing but this, however, should be stressed in speaking. There may be cases where the context or the circumstances are not sufficient to bring out the right meaning of a phrase. This is specially the case when through simplification of the spelling the lengthening of a verb vowel in the passive is not shown. In such cases only the verb form can show the real meaning of the phrase whether it is active or passive, as will be shown later.

36

## 2) The locative case.

Is the case which indicates where somebody is or from where he is coming. This rule also has its exceptions.

### a) Vowels change in the locative case as follows:

|   |  |  |  | Nominative | Locative  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  a | changes | into | ε | Marial | Mariel  |
|  i | changes | into | iε | Madiy | Madicy  |
|  e | changes | into | εε | Malek | Malzek  |
|  ε | changes | into | e | geu (town) | geu  |
|  o | changes | into | ɔ | Gok | Gök  |
|  ɔ | changes | into | a | Madhol | Madhal  |
|   |  |  |  | Korok | Karak  |
|  ou | changes | into | au | Atokthou | Atokthau  |
|  u | changes | into | uo | Apuk | Apuok  |
|  ui | changes | into | uiε | Maluil | Maluiel  |

Note: ca and ja become in the locative *cie* and *jiε*, e. g.: *Macar*, loc. *Macier*.

### b) Other nouns lengthen their vowel in the locative case.

E. g.: *Adhal* locative: *Adhaal*, *Abuok* locative: *Abuook*, *lom* (side) locative: *loom*, e. g.: *nyuc aloom* sit at my side.

Other nouns do not change at all. *Nyamlel*, *Abiei*, *yot* (hut), e. g.: *tik atɔ yot* the woman is in the hut.

### c) Irregular locative cases.

- *war* river, lake, well - locat. *wiir* in the river, into the river, belonging to the river
- *baai* village, home » *baai* at home, *beiː* home, from home
- *luak* byre » *luɛk* in the byre, into the byre
- *yac* belly » *yic* in the belly
- -ic suffix, in, inside
- *loyten* on this side
- *loytui* on the other side

Examples are:
- *nyap ato wiir* a crocodile is in the river
- *rian wiir* canoe, boat
- *koc wiir* people (spirits) of the river
- *kan wiir* bridge
- *yen arɛm yic* I have a pain in the belly
- *dhak atɔ wutic* the boys are in the cattle camp.
- *yok yic* among the cattle.
- *bɛɛi yic* in the villages

37

d) When two place names are united by *ku* (and) only the first one is put in the locat. case

e. g.: *Madiey ku Gogrial* at Mading and G.

e) Qualified names are not put in the locat. case.

E. g. *luay beny* in the chief's byre
*rou aliu war panda* there is no hippo in the river of our country

### 3) Cases for terms of time.

Only a few words have a special case to indicate «when» an event took place.

E. g. *thei* evening *theei* in the evening
*mai* winter *muoi* in the winter

Ker spring *Ruel* summer *Rut* autumn do not change.

#### Exercise 15.

|  Wa areer Madiey | My father stays at Mading  |
| --- | --- |
|  Yen aci jaal Gagriel | I left Gagrial  |
|  Miith aci piooc Kuajook | The children have been instructed at Kuajok  |
|  Rou aci riy wiir. | The hippo ran into the river  |
|  Muoc yeen riy lai | Give me meat of antelope.  |
|  Diar ku ror alui domic ku miethken ato baai | The women and the men are working in the field and their children are at home  |
|  Rut ku muoi koc ato tooc | In autumn and winter the people are in the swamp  |
|  Tony aci cap mec. | The pot is put on the fire  |

### 4. Qualified nouns

A. When a noun in the singular is followed by another noun or an adjective or by an explanatory phrase; the ending is liable to change as already shown in Part One.

The conjunction *ē* (very short) put between two nouns indicates relation of ownership or real dependence and in such cases it corresponds to the English of. In other cases the conjunction is omitted. E. g.: *wey ē Dut* Dut's cow. *manh ē beny* the chief's child. But *ē* is omitted in cases like *wun meth* (the child's father). Cp. ch. 33, Conjunctions.

38

B. *Proper-nouns, family names and surnames.*

1) If a person’s name is followed by the father’s name the first one may undergo ending change like common nouns when qualified.

Examples: *Thieg e Dut*
*Kuany e Bol*
Thiek son of Dut,
Kuac son of Bol.

Note. In documents it is convenient not to change the ending and to write the name in its original form.

2) *Surnames.* Most surnames are bull-names indicating a colour.

Note. A Dinka likes to take the name of his favourite bull as a surname. On the other hand it happens also that he may have a fancy for a certain name which he will take as a surname and he will try to get such a bull if he has not one of that colour.

A surname joined to one’s family name does not cause ending change nor is it preceded by the conjunction *e*. In addition the family name is more accentuated than the bull name but less accentuated than the father’s name. E.g.: *Kuac Malual* (Malual is the surname of Kuac). *Kuany e Malual* (Kuac son of Malual). (The more accentuated names are printed in bold types.

*Exceptions.* Several family names and other nouns do not change their ending in conformity with the rules given in Part One. Such names are: *Dut*, *Madut*, *Akot*, *Akot*, (Akot when used as female name may change the ending). *Dut e Gir*, *Akot e Toy*, *Akon e Bak* (Akot, daughter of Bak). Nouns. *Wet*, *rec*, *guop*, *kep*, *cok*, *thok*, *wai*, *luoi*.

E.g.: One will say

|  wet beny | the chief’s order | kau lacok | straight rafter  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  guop raan | the human body | luoi ril | a hard work  |
|  kep path | a nice song | kerac dit | a great evil  |
|  thok cuai | a fat goat | kerany dit | a big snake  |
|  wai baar | a long stick | cok lai | tracks of antelopes  |

Meth child becomes *manh* e.g. *manh e mony* this man’s son
Mou merissa becomes *muon* e.g. *muon kec* strong merissa

## 5. Verbs

### Auxiliary Verbs.

1) *ee pl. aa* before nouns means *to be*. When put before a verb in its mainform it gives the verb the meaning of the habitual.

E.g. *ee raon*
*aa Muonyjiey*
*koor ee koc cam*
*kek aa koc nok*

it is a person
they are Dinkas
the lion attacks people
they kill the people

39

The interrogative of ee (pl. aa) is ye

E. g. ye wur?
ee wa

is he your father?
he is my father.

The narrative of ee (pl. aa) is eye (pl. aake).

Examples: their war mony eye beny long ago this man was a chief
agok their war aake dhak long ago the monkeys were boys in
wutic a cattle camp.

Note. This auxiliary verb ee becomes a when the following word begins with a contracting in speaking both a's to a single long aa. Instead of saying ee amaal one says a-amaal (it is a sheep), a-ajith (it is a hen).

2) a- used as prefix gives the verb the meaning of a definite statement. This verb form which has the prefix a- is called the «Indicative Form». It does not contain an indication of time and is often, especially in a narrative, used for past actions.

Note. It is only because the particular patterns of European languages are fixed in our minds that we give to the purely indicative form the meaning of the present tense but it is not correct to do so even when the indicative of our present tense is given in Dinka by this form.

In compound tenses of the indicative verb this particle is put before these tense particles (aci, abi, aye).

Adjectives when used as verbs receive this prefix a- likewise.

E. g. raan path a nice man, raane apath this man is nice.

If the subject is a plural but not mentioned as in answers, the a lengthens into aa.

E. g. koc alui the people are working aalui (they) are working.

3) ci is the tense particle for the past tense,
bi is the tense particle for the future tense,
ye is the tense particle for the habitual.

In the indicative form these tense particles take the prefix a-.

Note. In a later chapter will be shown how these particles can merge with personal pronouns.

4) a repeated after the tense particle or after the object gives the verb the meaning of the habitual.

E. g. Yin abi gric 50 adom epei
Yin abi a banyda

you will get 50 piasters per month
you will be our chief. (In this case
«abi a» has the meaning of the
habitual future of «to be» ee)

40

5) ci when followed by a verb with the pure indicative form (without tense particle) or by a verb in the future tense, gives the phrase a negative meaning.

N. B. ci followed by a verb in mainform is the tense particle for the past tense with positive meaning. Strangely enough the ci has in both cases the same pronunciation. The positive or negative meaning is given only by the verb and its higher or lower accent.

E. g. aci koc nāk he does not harm the people
aci koc nāk he has harmed the people (See ch. Negat. sentences)

In the Dinka spelling rules it is suggested that ce he written for ci when otherwise the right meaning cannot be easily caught. But it is not convenient to put the distinction in the ci when it is in the verb.

6) kec (nearly kei) is the negative particle for the past tense. In the indicative it has the prefix a-.

E. g. Wa akec ben my father has not come.

7) cie (ci-ye) is the negative particle for the habitual (acie for the indicative).

E. g. yin acie koc muəc you don't make presents to the people. In another chapter it will be shown how these negative particles merge with personal pronouns.

8) ē (plur. aa) is the particle for the narrative. To the plural form aa shortened pronouns are joined (aao, aawe, aake).

Examples: watheer benydit ēto thin long ago there was a chief
kek aake ci math dōm they made friendship.

(As seen in this example the past tense particle ci may be joined to the narrative particle. Cp. ch. Narrative).

## Exercise 16.

Yin akec wetdie piy
Kockui aa Muonyjiey
Wun aci thou warakol.
Yen abi lo keny nhiak.

koc kok aye luui ku koc kok acie luui.
Yen abi luoydu piy luyn banyic a-kol da.
Yen acin weu, yen abi luui.
Yen aci yi bi kony epath.
Koor ee wey cam panda.
Yen aca-anyaar tiy warakol.
Wa eye luui geu.
Mony ee koc nok epath.

Lual ee beny theer, ebo cien ku aci loo tuey.
Koc karou aake ci lo keny pan Malual.

You did not obey my order.
Those people are Dinkas.
His father has died yesterday.
I shall set out for a journey tomorrow.
Some people are working and others are not working.
I shall hear your case in the chiefs court another time.
I am without money, I shall work.
I shall not help you without reason.
A lion attacks the cattle in our village.
Yesterday I saw a buffalo.
My father works in the town.
The fellow beats people without reason.
Lual is an ancient chief, he came from the east and proceeded towards the west.
Two people made a journey to the Malual country.

## 6. Verb forms

Two verb forms are most frequently used. First the verb with a long or half long vowel or diphthong. This form is never changed, only its tense particle may increase in length or merge with personal pronouns. This form will be called the Main form.

It is used in the narrative without any tense particle and it is used in the other tenses (past, future and habitual) with the tense particles ci bi ye and ee and after the auxiliary verbs. The second form has a short or half long vowel or diphthong (always shorter than that of its

42

mainform) and may be different from its mainform. This form is used with the prefix a- to indicate a definite statement. It will be called the Indicative, it does not include any indication of time.

From this form are derived - often with vowel change - the Imperative and Interrogative (with similar forms), the Conditional etc. as will be seen later.

The indicative is used also in the narrative after the conjunction ku.

In Table IV many examples of verbs with their mainform and indicative will be given.

## Exercise 17.

|  Tikydu ee manhde nhiaar apri. | Your wife loves her child very much.  |
| --- | --- |
|  Yen ajäl emene. | I am leaving now.  |
|  Benydit abi ben nhiak. | The chief will come tomorrow.  |
|  Kackia abo. | My people are coming.  |
|  Acin raan bi yi nok. | Nobody will harm you.  |
|  Cok anäk yeen. | Hunger kills me.  |
|  Wada aci lei nok. | Our father has killed an antelope.  |
|  Koc aci weuku tau tim cok. | The people put your luggage under the tree.  |
|  Beny aci polic tooc. | The chief has sent a policeman.  |
|  Wa atuc yeen. | My father sends me.  |
|  Luel wet yic. | Say the truth.  |
|  Raan abi piu bei. | A man will fetch water.  |
|  Piu abii. | The water is being brought.  |
|  Wa abo yakol. | My father will come today.  |
|  Benydit abi wet lek koc. | The chief will tell it to the people.  |
|  Koc apiy wetdu. | The people obey your order.  |
|  Duk rioc aci yi näk. | Do'nt fear he will not harm you.  |
|  Raan dit aci bo yakol abi ben nhiak. | The great man will not come today he will come tomorrow.  |

## 7. Possessive suffixes

The Dinka use suffixes where we use possessive pronouns (my, your etc.) as shown in Part One. These suffixes cause not only ending changes but also vowel changes in the nouns to which they are joined. Vowels which do not change in quality become shorter.

Such vowel changes take place also before the numeral tok (one) and the diminutive suffixe thi (plur. thii) = «small» or «tiny».

43

## Rules for vowel changes

|  e | becomes | ɛ or a | E. g. | yep, yɛmdie | my axe,  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|   |  |  | » | meth, manhdie | my son,  |
|   |  |  | » | meth, mathde | his friend.  |
|  i | becomes | iɛ or ie | » | tik, tityde | his wife,  |
|   |  |  | » | tiɛm tok | one tree.  |
|  o | becomes | ɔ or uɔ or ia | » | dom, duɔmde | his field,  |
|   |  |  | » | nhom, nhiamdu | your head.  |
|  o | becomes (breathed) | o or ɔ | » | cok, cokde (or: cakde) | his foot,  |
|   |  |  | » | joy, joydu | your dog,  |
|   |  |  | » | koor, kɔr tok | one lion.  |
|  ɔ | becomes | a | » | gol, galde | his clan,  |
|   |  |  | » | lɔm, lam tok | one rib.  |
|  u | becomes | uɔ or ia | » | wut, wuɔnda | our cattle camp.  |
|   |  |  | » | pur, piardie | my hoe.  |
|  uou | becomes | ia | » | puou, piandie | my heart.  |
|  ɔu | becomes | a or ia | » | rɔu, ran tok | one hippo,  |
|   |  |  | » | mɔu, miande | his merissa.  |
|  uo | becomes | uɔ | » | guop, guɔpde | his body.  |
|  uɔ | becomes | ia | » | muɔr, miarde | his bull.  |

These vowel changes take place also in plural nouns.

E. g. rin, riɛnkie
puou, piathkua
my name (rin is a plural form).
our hearts.

## Irregular forms.

|  wɔɪt | sou | wende | his son  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  wɛɛt | sous | watke | his sons.  |
|  baai | village | panda | our home  |
|  bɛɛi | villages | bɛɛiken | their villages.  |
|  muɔr | bull | miarde | his bull  |
|   |  | muɔrde | his mate.  |
|  moe | man, husband | muɔnydie | my husband  |
|   |  | tiŋ miaɛdie | a wife, another wife of my husb.  |
|  nya | daughter, girl | nyiandie | my daughter.  |
|  moe | man | mony (ɛ̃) | the man  |
|   |  | muɔny | this man, fellow.  |

Notes. 1) A noun with a suffix when qualified also by another noun or adjective or phrase, conserves its original vowel and takes an n after the lengthened suffix vowel.

44

Examples: tik wife, tieydu your wife,
tiyduun kor your second wife.
wey cow, weydu your cow,
weyduun ca yoc the cow you have bought.
miith children, miethkie my children,
miithkienka these my ch. home,
baai panden their home
pandeen theer their old home.

2) In cases of things owned together with other people the Dinka uses a plural suffix also where we would use the singular.

E. g. panda my village, but pandie my home.

3) These suffixes are sometimes used also separately like nouns.

E. g. dieen war (that which was once mine).

Duun war ei yi muor nhom? have you forgotten that matter or yours?
Dien (my dear).
Kony yeen dien help me my dear.

4) When names of the human body and its parts take the place of complements (not subjects) in a phrase, they do not take possessive suffixes but they take shortened pronouns as prefixes (a., yi., ye., o., we., ke-).

E. g. aguop on my body,
yiciin with your hand.
onhiim in front of us,
yin anhior apuou (I love you from my heart). But one says.
piandie aci riak my heart was rent (pandie is here subject).

## Exercise 18.

Ma acol rienke Adut.
Miardie adit ku miardu akoor.
Dhienhda acol Paker.
Yemdie arac.
Cok lan dit ato thin.
Kacku aa koc nhiar toy.
Koor ee riy wey nhiaar.
Diar acath kek miethken.

Coydit ato panda.
Pandun arac cok ato thin.
Banyda acol Lual Macar.

My mother is called Adut.
My bull is big and your bull is small.
Our clan is called Paker.
My axe is bad.
There are footprints of big game.
Your people like fighting.
The lion likes the meat of cattle.
The women are walking with their children.
There is great famine in our country.
Your country is bad there is famine.
Our chief is called Lual Macar.

45

Beny aci loo Wau kek wende.
The chief has gone to Wau. with his son.

Kacke aci loo beziken.
His people returned to their homes.

Tik ee manhde nhiaar.
The woman loves her child.

Bol ee manh e ma.
Bol is a son of my mother.

Tik aci manh moc dhieth.
The woman has given birth to a little boy.

Muoc mithkienka thook.
Give these my children something to eat (for their mouths).

Yin cath anhom ku koe kok abi caath akou.
You walk in front of me and the other people will walk behind me.

Muk kene yicin.
Hold this with your hands.

Acii kou gut yecok.
He pricked his foot with a thorn.

Aacik tip kenyin.
They have seen it with their own eyes.

Liecke wekoth.
Look behind you.

Note: In the Dinka Spelling Rules, it was agreed to write the suffixes quite distinctly from the noun. E. g. «manhde» but through assimilation one pronounces it «manhdhe». «Mathde» is pronounced «madhe». One writes «wetde» and pronounces «wedde».

## 8. Relationship terms

These relationship terms are, as already has been shown in Part One, concrete terms, i. e. they have a positive meaning, e. g. «my father» not «father» generically. In place of our terms, when without possessive adjective, the third person is used

48

E. g. Nhialic e Wun
wun meth
warken mithka

God the Father,
the child's father,
these children's fathers.

wadit,

{

niner,

}

my cousin.

walen, pl. walenkie,
panerda,

my pat. relation.
my mat. relation.
my brother in law (of a husband)
my sister in law (of a husband).

| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |

|  your | wur | mor | wamuth | nyankui  |
|  his, her | wun | man | wamenh | nyanken  |
|  our | wada | mada | wamathda | nyankaida  |

|   | pat. uncle | mat. uncle | his wife | pat. aunt | mat. aunt  |

Notes. 1) Instead of wamaath (my brother) one says also menkai pl. mithakai.

2) Tig war (not: tig wa) one of my father's (or my pat. uncle's) wives. Wen war = my father's (or my pat. uncle's) son.

3) The phrase yin wa (with short a) used in addressing a person also a boy, does not mean «my father» but «my friend» or «my dear». Wamath (both a's are short) has also a similar meaning. Also the phrase yin ma (with short a) used in addressing women and girls means «my dear».

## Exercise 19.

Wamathkie apur nyuom roor.
Nyirakai aci rap lo pon.
Wa kek wamaath ato luek.
Col mor ya?
Ma acol Acol.
Yakkua ato liil.
Wun apath apei.
Rin wamenh acol Dut.
Wada ke mada aliu baai.
Nyanken aci thiaak weg 30.
Wamathkua abiok yok.
Pig wet wur ke mor.
Wun kek man aci thou.
Ee manh walen.
Ee wen niner, wun kek ma ee gek kek nyaken.

My brothers are cultivating simsim in the forest.
My sisters went to weed the durra.
My father and my brother are in the byre.
How is your mother called?
My mother is called Acol.
Our cattle are in the swamp.
His father is very nice.
His brother is called Dut.
Our parents are absent.
His sister was married for 30 cows.
Our brothers herd cattle.
Obey your parents.
His parents have died.
He is my cousin.
He is my cousin, my mother is his father's sister.

5 - Dinka Grammar.

50

# 9. Sex Indication

The Dinka language has no grammatical sex for nouns and pronouns. The different sexes of men and animals are indicated by different nouns. In the case of animals the sex name may be joined to the animal's name.

1) For persons there are the following terms.

|  moc | man, husband. | tik | woman, wife.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  dhok | boy. | nya | girl.  |
|  aparak | boy with head scarification. | nyambim | maiden.  |
|  rienythi | young man. | nyan mathiay | aged girl.  |
|  manh moc | little boy. | manh nya | little girl.  |

2) Sex names for cattle and other animals.

|  muor | bull. | wey | cow.  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  muor thon | bull (for breeding). | manh wey | calf.  |
|  muor buoc | castrated bull. | dou | heifer.  |
|  gar | male cob. | thil | female cob.  |
|  maluith | young male cob. |  |   |

The sex of other animals is indicated by the names

muor (pl. mior) or thon (pl. thon) male and
yuot (pl. yut) female.

These sex names are put after the animal's name

E. g.: anyaar e yuot a buffalo cow.

If the animal is qualified also in another way the sex name is put first

E. g.: muor akon dit a big elephant bull.

The names for young animals are formed by putting manh before the animal's name in the case of a young male and nyan for a young female,

E. g.: manh mabior a white bull calf, and nyan yar white heifer.

3) Cattle-names (applied also to other animals) derived from their colours or other peculiarities have in most cases the prefix ma- for males and the prefix a- for females.

E. g.: macar a black bull acol a black cow.

Note. Such names are adopted as family-names and surnames, but not all family-names beginning with A- are female names, e. g. Akot, Akot, Akon, Akol etc. are male names.

51

|  mabior | mibior | yar | yoor | white;  |
